ii
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
The share of intermittent renewable energy sources (IRES), such as wind and solar, is
constantly increasing in the energy mix as many countries aim for a net carbon neutral
future. With the variability and unpredictability of IRES, the problem of mismatch in de­
mand and supply arises. A promising way to counter the imbalances is to utilize chemical
energy, in the form of synthetic natural gas (SNG), produced by a Power­to­gas (PtG)
system. The purpose of this study was to analyze different PtG systems, and see how
they compared in technical and economic performance. Three different systems of pro­
duction of SNG were analyzed. All systems consisted of a solid oxide electrolysis cell
(SOEC) and catalytic reactors where different operation modes of SOEC were consid­
ered; atmospheric steam electrolysis, pressurized steam electrolysis, and pressurized
co­electrolysis with internal methane formation. A zero­dimensional modeling tool, Dy­
namic Network Analysis (DNA), was used to simulate the systems to obtain their charac­
teristics for the analysis. Technical evaluation indicated that increased operation pressure
resulted in a better plant efficiency. The system that showed the best electricity to SNG
efficiency was the co­electrolysis system with 82.0%, followed by the pressurized system
(76.3%) and lastly the atmospheric system (73.2%). Economic evaluation determined
the production cost of SNG, mainly consisting of capital cost and cost of electricity. In­
creased operating pressure led to a higher capital cost, resulting in the highest production
cost for the pressurized system. The lowest production cost, 143.02 €/MWhSNG, was ob­
tained by the co­electrolysis system even though it had the highest capital cost. The high
efficiency of the system decreased the cost of electricity and therefore outweighed the
high capital cost. The results of the technical and economic evaluation indicated that the
co­electrolysis system is the most promising system analyzed.
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis

==PAGE:14==
1
Introduction
In 2019 the European Commission introduced a package of policy initiatives called The
European Green Deal (EGD) that was then approved in the following year. One of the
main goal of the deal is to make the European Union (EU) net carbon neutral by the year
2050. An additional milestone is to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions by 55% in
2030 compared to 1990 levels. Renewable energy is set to play a fair role in these goals
[1]. The increased share of renewables in the European energy system will, to a large
extent, consist of increased power generation from wind and solar. Both wind and solar
fall under the definition of variable renewable energy (VRE) which means that the power
generation will depend on the weather conditions. The variability in weather can lead
to differences in demand and supply of energy, and therefore electrical energy storage
(EES) systems are needed to balance out the mismatches [2]. The EES capacity needed
for Europe to be able to cope with the large share of VRE is said to be between 12% and
15% of the annual consumption [3].
Currently, 96% of all the storage capacity in the world is in the form of pumped­hydro
storage (PHS). The reason why PHS dominates the EES market is low cost, high capac­
ity, and high efficiency. The downside of PHS is that it is limited by geological factors
and therefore it is not suitable for all terrains [2]. Another form of EES technology, that
can match the storage capacity of the PHS, is called Power­to­gas (PtG). The concept
of PtG is to use electricity to produce hydrogen that can then be converted to methane.
In other words, electrical energy is converted to chemical energy [4]. Both hydrogen and
methane can be considered as the final energy carrier. The challenge of storing pure
hydrogen makes the methane pathway a more promising option for large scale energy
storage. Another advantage of storing the energy in the form of methane is that the stor­
age infrastructure is already in place. In Europe, the gas grid currently has a storage
capacity of 1139.8 TWh, which is close to one third of Europe’s annual gas consumption
[5]. Figure 1.1 displays the different properties of different EES technologies.
Figure 1.1: Comparison of different EES technologies [6].
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
1
==PAGE:15==
Methane that is synthesized by using renewable electricity is also known as synthetic
natural gas (SNG). SNG is considered an alternative to natural gas (NG) as they have very
similar properties and composition. SNG production has two pathways and the difference
between them is the source of carbon used for the synthesis. The sourced carbon for the
process either comes from biological processes, biological methanation, or in the form of
captured carbon dioxide CO2 from industry or the atmosphere, catalytic methanation [7].
This study will only focus on the catalytic methanation process.
The production of SNG via catalytic methanation can be divided into two process steps.
The first step is the production of renewable hydrogen via electrolysis. Currently, three
electrolysis technologies are considered suitable for the process. The following technolo­
gies are: alkaline electrolysis (AEL), polymer electrolyte membrane electrolysis (PEM),
and solid oxide electrolysis cell (SOEC). The second step of the process is methane syn­
thesis where the the produced hydrogen and CO2 react to form methane in the presence
of a catalyst [8]. A schematic of the catalytic methanation process is displayed in fig. 1.2.
Figure 1.2: Schematic of the catalytic methanation process.
The methanation process is very exothermic which makes the integration with SOEC
promising. The SOEC is a high temperature electrolysis process that utilizes heat to
reduce the electricity demand for hydrogen production. The integration of heat from the
methane synthesis with the SOEC results in high overall efficiency SNG production [9].
The combination of SOEC and catalytic methanation can also provide a slightly different
process for SNG production. The SOEC can be operated at elevated pressure and fed
with water and CO2 resulting in internal methanation in the SOEC. This co­electrolysis
operation can result in an even better overall efficiency of SNG production [10]. Due to
the promising outcome of SOEC operation with methane synthesis, this combination will
be the focus of the study.
Few studies have looked into pressurized co­electrolysis operation for internal methane
production. Jensen et al. [11] conducted a pressurized co­electrolysis experiment on
a SOEC stack. The stack was operated at 18.7 bar pressure and 700 °C resulting in
a produce gas with 18% methane content. Bernadet et al. [12] looked into the effects
of pressure on co­electrolysis operation. Co­electrolysis at 800 °C was performed at at­
mospheric pressure and elevated pressure of 10 bar. The analysis of the produced gas
showed that methane is only formed at 10 bar and the share of methane in the gas did
not exceed 6%.
Alongside experimentation on pressurized co­electrolysis, many studies have modeled
the process with downstream methane synthesis and conducted a techno­economic anal­
ysis of the system. Ebbehøj [13] modeled the production of SNG with co­electrolysis with
2
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:16==
additional methane upgrading after the electrolysis. Direct air capture (DAC) technology
was also included in the model for the carbon source. The SOEC was operated at 80
atm and 850 °C resulting in 10.2% methane content at the outlet of the fuel electrode.
The techno­economic analysis resulted in an energy efficiency of 58% based on Lower
heating value (LHV) and the production cost of SNG was around 190 €/MWhSNG.
Giglio et al. [10] compared the performance of SNG production where one system op­
erates a SOEC with steam electrolysis and the other system operates a SOEC with co­
electrolysis. Both systems are operated at 850°C and 33.1 bar. The results indicated that
the co­electrolysis system gives better performance than the system based on the steam
electrolysis. The efficiency of the co­electrolysis is calculated to be 81.4% but the one for
the steam electrolysis is 76%.
Salomone et al. [14] created a techno­economic model for a PtG system based on SOEC
water electrolysis and CO2 methanation. The electricity for the model was obtained from
an electric grid that is entirely based on renewable energy sources. The model calculated
the energy efficiency and a range of levelized costs of production of SNG for the year
2050. The results from the model give the overall energy efficiency of 77% for the PtG
system. The levelized cost of production is given in the range of 60.2 ­ 224.9 €/MWhSNG
as different electricity prices are used for the calculations.
Götz et al. [8] made a technological and economical review on PtG operation. A part of
the review gathers the production cost of PtG systems that are operated for 3000 hours
per year. The presented range of production cost in the review is 40 ­ 392 €/MWh.
1.1
The goal of the study
The aim of this study is to make a techno­economic comparison of the production of SNG
with three different designs of a PtG system. All systems will consist of SOEC and down­
stream methane synthesis. One design considers pressurized co­electrolysis operation
of the SOEC. The other two designs will operate a SOEC with steam electrolysis but the
difference in the designs is the operating pressure in the SOEC. One will be operated at
atmospheric pressure and the other at elevated pressure. The designs are compared in
system efficiency as well as in production cost.
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
3
==PAGE:17==
1.2
Outline of the thesis
The structure of the thesis is the following:
Chapter 1 ­ Introduction. States the challenge that comes with the increased share of
intermittent renewable energy sources in the energy mix and how the PtG concept can
counter it. An overview of findings from technical and/or economic analysis of PtG sys­
tems based on SOEC is given and the goal of the project is stated.
Chapter 2 ­ Technology information. The fundamentals of the SOEC and catalytic metha­
nation are covered. The main components of SOEC and the basic thermodynamics are
described. Two different operation modes, steam electrolysis and co­electrolysis, are in­
troduced and the main reactions for each operation are displayed. The effect of elevated
pressure on the operation modes is discussed and the problem with deposition of car­
bon during co­electrolysis is stated. Finally, the reactions and operating conditions of the
catalytic methanation are described.
Chapter 3 ­ Methodology. States the comparable scope for the analysis and provides
a detailed description of the process designs. The software used to develop the PtG
systems is introduced and the process design parameters are defined. A definition of the
electricity to SNG efficiency is provided as it is the main focus of the technical evaluation.
The economic evaluation is based on the levelized cost of production of SNG. The process
of obtaining the production cost is described.
Chapter 4 ­ Results and discussion. Contains the results of the technical and economical
evaluations of the three different systems that are analyzed. The chapter is divided into
two main sections. One where the technical results of each system are displayed and then
compared. The other part shows the results from the economic evaluation, one system
at a time, and compares the findings.
Chapter 5 ­ Conclusion. The main findings of the study are concluded and further works
and development of the topic are stated.
4
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:18==
2
Technology information
A PtG plant that is based on catalytic methanation consists of two main processes. The
first process is the production of hydrogen with renewable electricity. Renewable hydro­
gen is produced with a technique called electrolysis where applied voltage and current,
drive reduction and oxidation reactions. The half­reactions that occur in the electrolysis
process differ based on the type of technology used [15]. The component used for elec­
trolysis in this study is a solid oxide electrolyzer that consists of stacks of SOEC. As the
hydrogen is not the end product of the system the second process is required where the
hydrogen is reacted with CO2 to form SNG.
2.1
SOEC
The SOEC is an electrochemical device that is operated at high temperatures, usually
between 600 and 850°C. The device consists of three main components, two porous
electrodes, a cathode and an anode, and an electrolyte that separates electrodes. The
electrolyte acts as an ionic conductor where oxygen ions (O2−) move from one electrode
to the other. The electrodes are connected with an electric conducting material to enable
electron transport [16]. During electrolysis, fuel is supplied to the cathode where it is
reduced and oxygen ions are formed. Therefore, the cathode can also be referred to as
the fuel electrode. The oxygen ions migrate across the electrolyte to the anode where
the ions are oxidized and form pure oxygen (O2). Since the only substance formed at the
anode is oxygen, the anode can also be referred to as the oxygen electrode [13].
2.1.1
Materials
As the name of the technique implies, the components of the SOEC are based on ce­
ramic materials. The most common design is where the electrodes and the electrolyte
are based on yttria­stabilized zirconia (YSZ) [16]. The electrodes need to have ionic and
electric conducting properties so the material used is often a ceramic­metallic compos­
ite of YSZ, where the YSZ acts as the ionic conductor and the metal provides electric
conductivity. As mentioned earlier, the purpose of the electrolyte is to conduct the ions
between the electrodes so it should only have ionic conducting properties. Therefore,
pure ceramic material like YSZ is suitable for the electrolyte. Ebbehøj et al. in [13] looked
into the production of SNG where syngas is produced via pressurized co­electrolysis in
a solid oxide electrolyzer. The fuel electrode in that system is a Ni/YSZ composite, and
the oxygen electrode is a blend of YSZ and lanthanum­doped manganite (LSM), where
the LSM is a ceramic material that acts as an electronic conductor. The electrolyte in
Ebbehøj’s device consists of pure YSZ. The SOEC that is modeled in this study is not as­
signed to a specific design of a solid oxide electrolyzer, but the design described earlier
is suitable for the simulated process.
2.1.2
Triple phase boundaries
It is worth mentioning that the reactions in the electrolysis process mainly occur at spe­
cific sites in the electrodes. The active sites for the reactions are located at junctions in
the electrode where the gas is able to reach an ionic conducting phase and an electric
conducting phase. These sites are also called the triple phase boundaries (TPB) [17] and
are displayed in fig. 2.1.
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
5
==PAGE:19==
Figure 2.1: Triple phase boundaries explained [18].
2.1.3
Thermodynamics of SOEC
Reversible voltage
In order to drive the cell reactions in electrolysis, external energy needs to be supplied
to the system. The energy needed to run an ideal electrolysis cell, where no losses are
present, is given by the change in the Gibbs free energy for the overall reaction of the cell.
This value can then be used to calculate the reversible voltage of the cell with eq. (2.1).
The reversible voltage is also known as the Nernst voltage.
Erev = −∆G
ne · F
(2.1)
∆G in eq. (2.1) is the change in Gibbs free energy, F is the Faradays constant and ne
indicates the number of electrons transferred in the reaction. The reversible voltage is
the voltage when no current is drawn to the cell and no reactions are observed in the cell.
Another term that can be used for the reversible voltage is open circuit voltage (OCV).
When a different voltage than the OCV is applied to the cell a current will flow through
the system where the magnitude of the current depends on the applied voltage. The
relation between the applied voltage and measured current can be plotted in a so­called
polarization curve that is also known as the current­voltage curve or IV­curve for short.
In these graphs, the cell voltage is plotted as a function of current density. The current
density is defined as the current applied to the system divided by the active area of the
cell. The equation of the current density is displayed in eq. (2.2).
i = Icell
A
(2.2)
Here i stands for the current density, Icell represents the current drawn by the system and
A is the active cell area of the system. In figure fig. 2.2 the polarization curves of three
different designs of solid oxide cells (SOCs) are presented. The graph displays the cell
voltage for both electrolysis and fuel cell mode of the SOCs, the positive current densities
correspond to fuel cell operation and the negative correspond to electrolysis operation.
6
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:20==
Figure 2.2: Polarization curve for 3 different SOCs at 800°C with 50%H2O­50%H2 fuel
composition [19].
Overpotentials and Thermoneutral voltage
In a perfect system without losses, the cell voltage is always equal to the reversible volt­
age. However, all systems have losses that reduce the performance of the system. The
losses in SOEC lead to higher cell voltage required in order to draw a specific amount
of current. The additional voltage required is defined as the overpotential and it can be
split up into different types of losses. The total overpotential of the cell (ηcell) is defined
by eq. (2.3).
ηcell = ηact + ηohm + ηcon
(2.3)
The ηact stands for the activation losses, ηohm for ohmic losses and ηcon for concentration
losses [20]. With the ηcell defined the operating cell voltage (Vcell) can be given with
eq. (2.4).
Vcell = Erev + ηcell
(2.4)
In fig. 2.3 the contribution of each overpotential in eq. (2.3) is shown. The current density
shown on the x­axis in fig. 2.3 is positive but that is inconsistent with fig. 2.2 where the
current density is negative for electrolysis mode. The author of fig. 2.3 uses a different
approach to the polarization curve where positive current density represents electrolysis
mode and negative current density represents the fuel cell mode. To avoid confusion and
to be consistent with fig. 2.2 the x­axis on fig. 2.3 should show the negative values of the
corresponding current density values.
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
7
==PAGE:21==
Figure 2.3: Polarization curve of SOEC indicating the contribution of different overpoten­
tials [20].
Another way to define the ηcell is to allocate the losses to each component of the cell so
the total overpotential can also be displayed with eq. (2.5):
ηcell = ηanode + ηelectrolyte + ηcathode
(2.5)
where ηanode and ηcathode stands for the losses occurring at each electrode and ηelectrolyte
represents the losses related to the electrolyte.
The electrolysis process is endothermic and if external heat is not supplied to the cell a
temperature variation will occur within the cell [21]. However, external heating can be
avoided by increasing the operating voltage of the cell to a voltage defined as the ther­
moneutral voltage (VTN). At thermoneutral voltage the heat demand needed to prevent
temperature variation in the cell is covered by the Joule heat that is generated by the in­
ternal resistance of the cell [22]. The increase in voltage to get the isothermic operation
of the cell is defined as ηq and then the thermoneutral voltage is given with eq. (2.6).
VTN = Erev + ηcell + ηq
(2.6)
If the cell is operated at a voltage greater than the thermoneutral voltage the additional
overpotential (ηheating) results in more Joule heat generated and heat would be released
to the surrounding environment [22]. With this information eq. (2.4) can be updated to
eq. (2.7) to consider the losses due to the additional Joule heating.
Vcell = Erev + ηcell + ηq + ηheating
(2.7)
It needs to be considered that ηheating only exists when Vcell > VTN.
Operating voltage and area specific resistance
All the different overpotentials here above make the calculation of the operating cell volt­
age quite complex. The calculation can be simplified by utilizing the area specific resis­
tance (ASR). The ASR is determined from a polarization curve for the system used. The
polarization curve forms a linear region around the voltage axis and the slope of the line
is the ASR. Therefore the approximation for the operating cell voltage can be written as
a function of the current density. The approximation is given by eq. (2.8).
Vcell = Erev −ASR(p, T) · i
(2.8)
8
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:22==
It must be noted that the ASR is dependent on the operating temperature and pressure
since different operating conditions will generate different polarization curves [13].
Thermal management of the SOEC
The operation of SOEC at thermoneutral voltage eliminates the challenge of temperature
variation. The downside of operating at the thermoneutral voltage is that it results in fast
degradation of the cell. Skafte et al. [21], performed an experiment where a SOEC was
operated in AC:DC operation instead of the conventional galvanostatic or potentiostatic
operation. This operation differs from the conventional one in a way that, instead of oper­
ating the cell at a constant direct current, rapid reverse pulses are applied to the constant
direct current at fixed time intervals. To be specific, the pulses were applied to the sys­
tem every 30 ms and each pulse lasted for 3 ms. During these pulses, the cell rapidly
switches from electrolysis mode to fuel cell mode. An illustration of both operations is
shown in fig. 2.4.
Figure 2.4: AC:DC operation demonstration [21].
The reason for the alternating operation mode is to compensate for the endothermic re­
action heat that occurs during the electrolysis mode with the exothermic fuel cell mode.
The outcome of the experiment is that the degradation rate for the AC:DC operation is
significantly lower than the one operated in DC mode. Even though the production rate of
hydrogen in the AC:DC operation is lower due to lower average current density the much
longer lifetime will be more beneficial than the higher production rate.
2.1.4
Water electrolysis
Water electrolysis via SOEC is the process of splitting water into hydrogen and oxygen.
The water is fed to the fuel electrode in the form of steam. At the fuel electrode, the water
is reduced to hydrogen and oxygen ions. The reduction half­reaction occurring at the fuel
side is described with reaction 2.9.
2H2O + 4e−−→2H2 + 2O2−
(2.9)
The oxygen ions move across the electrolyte to the oxygen electrode where they are
oxidized into oxygen. The oxidation half­reaction is described with reaction 2.10.
2O2−−→O2 + 4e−
(2.10)
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
9
==PAGE:23==
The half­reactions given in reactions 2.9 and 2.10 can be combined to form the total re­
action of the electrolysis and the reaction is given with reaction 2.11.
2H2O −→2H2 + O2
(2.11)
A schematic of water electrolysis in SOEC can be seen in fig. 2.5. This process does not
require any feed to the oxygen electrode and the only thing leaving that side is the formed
oxygen. This process can also be performed at elevated pressures up to 30 bar [23].
Figure 2.5: Schematic of SOEC water electrolysis [24].
2.1.5
Co­electrolysis
Co­electrolysis is very similar to the water electrolysis described earlier. The main differ­
ence is that the fuel, that is fed to the fuel electrode, is a mixture of steam and CO2. The
water undergoes the same reaction as in the water electrolysis or reaction 2.9 and the
CO2 is reduced to carbon monoxide (CO) and an oxygen ion. The reduction half­reaction
for the CO2 is given by reaction 2.12.
2CO2 + 4e−−→2CO + 2O2−
(2.12)
Just like in the water electrolysis the oxygen ions cross the electrolyte and undergo re­
action 2.10 to form oxygen. Another reaction that is also present at the fuel electrode is
the water­gas­shift (WGS) reaction. At elevated temperatures, like the ones present in
SOEC, the WGS equilibrium favors the reverse reaction or the reaction of hydrogen and
CO2 to form CO and water. The reverse water­gas­shift (rWGS) reaction is described
with reaction 2.13. Now the the fed in CO2 has two pathways to be converted to CO.
However, the rWGS reaction is responsible for the majority of the conversion of CO2 due
to the fact that the system operates at a cell voltage corresponding to steam electrolysis
or slightly below the voltage required for the reduction of CO2 [25].
CO2 + H2 ⇀
↽CO + H2O
(2.13)
2.1.6
Pressurized operation of SOEC
The reversible voltage presented in eq. (2.1) does not take into account the pressure of
the system. So to calculate the reversible voltage at elevated pressure eq. (2.14) can be
used.
Erev = E0 −RT
neF · ln
(
PH2 · P
1/2
O2
P
1/2
0
· PH2O
)
(2.14)
10
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:24==
Here E0 is the standard potential, R is the ideal gas constant, T is the operating tempera­
ture in Kelvin and P0 is the standard pressure. The other pressure elements indicate the
partial pressure of hydrogen, oxygen, and water [26]. In theory, an increase in pressure
results in an increased reversible voltage. Jensen et al. in 2010 [27] looked into the per­
formance of SOEC at different pressures. The experiment indicates that the performance
of the SOEC is briefly affected by the operating pressure. The results match the theory in
the way that the reversible voltage increases with increased pressure. However, the in­
creased pressure also decreases the internal resistance of the SOEC resulting in a lower
ASR. So the overall effect of elevated pressure operation on SOEC is small.
The elevated pressure does not affect the rWGS so it seems that the operation of water or
co­electrolysis is not sensitive to pressure. However, elevated pressure can be beneficial
for co­electrolysis in a way that the produced syngas can undergo a catalytic reaction to
form methane (CH4) within the cell. The fuel electrode can be equipped with a relevant
catalyst, like nickel­catalyst, that promotes CH4 formation when operating at elevated
pressure. The conversion of the syngas to CH4 is given with reaction 2.15. The conver­
sion of syngas to CH4 is very exothermic and the heat released by the reaction can result
in a lower thermoneutral voltage [28]. Figure 2.6 shows a schematic of co­electrolysis
where CH4 is formed within the cell.
CO + 3H2 −→CH4 + H2O
(2.15)
Figure 2.6: Schematic of pressurized SOEC co­electrolysis.
2.1.7
Carbon formation
During the operation of co­electrolysis at elevated pressure the hydrogen to carbon molar
(H/C) ratio needs to be considered. The reason is that the nickel­catalyst at the fuel
electrode can cause the formation of solid carbon at the fuel electrode. The formation of
carbon has a negative effect on the performance of the cell. The formation of solid carbon
is described with reaction 2.16, also known as the Boudouard reaction [28].
2CO(g) −→CO2(g) + C(s)
(2.16)
The minimum H/C ratio to avoid the formation of solid carbon depends on operating pres­
sure and temperature. The H/C ratio can be calculated with eq. (2.17) where it uses the
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
11
==PAGE:25==
molar flows of the compounds in the system. The minimum H/C ratio as a function of op­
erating pressure is given in fig. 2.7 for both electrolysis and fuel cell operation for different
temperatures.
H/C = 2 ˙nH2 + 2 ˙nH2O + 4 ˙nCH4
˙nCH4 + ˙nCO + ˙nCO2
(2.17)
Figure 2.7: Minimum H/C ratio for SOEC to avoid carbon formation against the operating
pressure [28].
2.2
Catalytic methanation
Catalytic methanation is the process where CO2 and CO react with hydrogen to form CH4
in the presence of a catalyst, where nickel is the most common choice. The conversion of
CO2 to CH4 or so­called CO2 methanation is displayed with reaction 2.18. This reaction
is also known as the Sabatier reaction. The conversion of CO to CH4 is referred to as CO
methanation and is described with reaction 2.15 in section 2.1.6, but that is the reverse
process of steam reforming [29].
CO2 + 4H2 −→CH4 + 2H2O
(2.18)
The performance of the methanation process is highly affected by the composition of the
syngas. A specific equation has been derived from stoichiometry in the methanation reac­
tions here above. The equation aims to fulfill the stoichiometric ratios in both methanation
reactions that is for every mole of CO2 there are 4 moles of hydrogen and for every mole
of CO there are 3 moles of hydrogen. The derived equation is displayed in eq. (2.19).
˙nH2 −˙nCO2
˙nCO + ˙nCO2
= 3
(2.19)
This equation is also valid if the syngas only consist of hydrogen and CO or hydrogen and
CO2. This ratio is often referred to as the feed module (FM) [13].
Both the CO and CO2 methanation are exothermic processes and therefore the reac­
tions favor lower temperatures. However, the operating temperature can not be too low
in order to have sufficient catalyst activity. The process cannot be operated at too high
a temperature since that can cause sintering and catalyst deactivation. Therefore, the
operation temperature is usually within the range of 200­500°C. By increasing the temper­
ature the conversion to CH4 in both processes decreases. The decrease in conversion
12
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:26==
can partly be compensated by increasing the pressure. Both reactions result in fewer
gaseous molecules, so by increasing the pressure the conversion should be increased
according to Le Chateliers’s principle. High pressures can also be harmful to the catalyst
and therefore a typical operating pressure for methanation is 10­30 atm [30]. In fig. 2.8
the effect of temperature and pressure on the conversion of the relevant carbon oxides
for both types of methanation with optimal stoichiometric ratios is displayed.
(a) CO methanation.
(b) CO2 methanation
Figure 2.8: Conversion of CO and CO2 based on temperature and pressure [31].

3
Methodology
3.1
Comparable scope
Due to the fact that the systems have different configurations and different operating con­
ditions a comparable scope needs to be established. One way to compare the systems
is to design the systems in a way that the end product of each system will be a stream
that has a similar molar composition at a specific temperature and pressure. This can
be achieved by having the same mass flow of CO2 fed into all the systems. The CO2 is
the only source of carbon in the process and is therefore a constraint on the maximum
production of methane. This can be described with eq. (3.1).
˙nCO2,in = ˙nCH4,max−out
(3.1)
All the carbon that enters the system will leave with the produced SNG stream mostly, if
not all, in the form of methane. The rest of the carbon will be in the form of unconverted
carbon oxides but their share is so small that it can be neglected. This approach results in
almost identical end­product streams in all systems even though the operating conditions
and configuration of the systems differ. The source of feedstocks and electricity is also a
part of the comparable scope. For a fair comparison of the systems, the same feedstocks
and source of electricity shall be considered.
The simplified box flow diagrams displayed in fig. 3.1 can give a better understanding of
the comparable scope. The same amount of CO2, from the same source, is supplied
to the system (marked with red in the figure). The carbon is then processed to a SNG
stream with a certain pressure and temperature to be fit for injection into the natural gas
grid (marked with green in the figure). The model for each system has specific process
design parameters that are used to generate the rest of the system characteristics, based
on the information stated earlier. The process design parameters of each system will be
covered in section 3.4.
14
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:28==
Figure 3.1: Simplified block flow diagrams of the three systems
3.2
Technical evaluation
The technical performance of a system is measured with the electricity to SNG efficiency.
The efficiency indicates how much of the electricity supplied to the system has been con­
verted to chemical energy in the form of SNG. This efficiency is often called the PtG
efficiency (ηPtG) and is described with equation eq. (3.2).
ηPtG = ˙mSNG · LHVSNG −˙mCO2 · LHVCO2
| ˙WSOEC + ˙WBOP |
(3.2)
Here ˙mSNG is the mass flow of the produced SNG in the process and LHVSNG is the
corresponding lower heating value. ˙mCO2 is the mass­flow of CO2 into the systems and
LHVCO2. ˙WSOEC is the electric power consumed by the SOEC and ˙WBOP is the electric
power consumed the balance­of­plant (BOP). Since CO2 is an inert gas, the LHV is zero.
Thus eq. (3.2) can be further simplified to eq. (3.3).
ηPtG =
˙mSNG · LHVSNG
| ˙WSOEC + ˙WBOP |
(3.3)
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
15
==PAGE:29==
The efficiency of the SOEC can also be determined in a similar way and is represented
by eq. (3.4):
ηSOEC = ˙mSOECout · LHVSOECout −˙mSOECin · LHVSOECin
| ˙WSOEC|
(3.4)
where ˙mSOECout is the mass­flow out of the SOEC at the fuel electrode and LHVSOECout
is the LHV of that stream. ˙mSOECin represents the mass flow of the stream at the inlet of
the fuel electrode and LHVSOECin is the corresponding LHV.
3.3
Economic evaluation
The main focus of the economic evaluation is to calculate the production cost of the SNG
in the three systems. The production cost is highly dependent on the investment cost of
the technologies. Some calculations are required to obtain the total investment cost of
a system. Therefore, the investment cost calculations are covered in a separate section
from the production cost calculations.
3.3.1
Investment cost evaluation
The investment cost, or the capital expenditures (CAPEX) of a system will be based on the
specific investment cost of the two main technologies of the process, SOEC, and catalytic
methanation. Both technologies have sources for the CAPEX on a plant size level so BOP
is included in the numbers. The specific investment cost is based on a specific capacity
of the technologies and in order to calculate the CAPEX for the capacity present in the
systems, the economies of scale need to be taken into account. The total CAPEX of the
SOEC and the catalytic methanation is then calculated with eq. (3.5).
CAPEXtechnology,C2 = CPtechnology · C1 ·
(C2
C1
)sf,technology
(3.5)
Here CAPEXtechnology,C2 stands for the total CAPEX for the given technology with the
capacity of C2 (can be SOEC or catalytic methanation), CPtechnology is the specific invest­
ment cost of the technology, C1 is the capacity of the technology corresponding to the
specific investment cost and sf, technology is the scaling factor for the technology.
When looking into the CAPEX of the SOEC it is more relevant to look at the cost as a
function of cell area. The reason is that the cell area indicates how many units of SOEC are
required for the system. The CAPEX calculated with eq. (3.5) is assumed to be for a SOEC
that is operated at atmospheric pressure. The CAPEX for the system with the SOEC
operated at atmospheric pressure can be calculated based on eq. (3.5) but the other two
systems will have different cell areas, so the CAPEX calculated for the atmospheric SOEC
is used to generate a specific investment cost per square meter. The specific investment
cost per square meter is given with eq. (3.6):
CASOEC,m2 = CAPEXSOEC,Catm
ASOEC,Catm
(3.6)
where CASOEC,m2 is the specific investment cost of SOEC per square meter,
CAPEXSOEC,Catm is the CAPEX for the SOEC operated at atmospheric pressure and
ASOEC,Catm is the cell area of the atmospheric SOEC. Now the equation for the CAPEX
determination of the SOEC is given with eq. (3.7). The ASOEC is the cell area of the
SOEC.
CAPEXSOEC = CASOEC,m2 · ASOEC
(3.7)
The total CAPEX of each system is then given with the sum of the CAPEX of the SOEC
and the catalytic methanation.
CAPEXtot = CAPEXSOEC + CAPEXCM
(3.8)
16
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:30==
3.3.2
Production cost evaluation
The levelized cost of production (LCOP) will be given in the unit €/MWhSNG. The calcu­
lation of the LCOP takes the annual cost of production of the SNG and divides it by the
annual production of the SNG in order to get the cost of producing one MWh of SNG. The
equation for the LCOP is given with eq. (3.9):
LCOP = crf · CAPEXtot + OPEXvar + OPEXfix
ESNG
(3.9)
where crf stands for the capital recovery factor that indicates the annual contribution of
the CAPEX concerning the production cost of the SNG. The value for crf is determined
with eq. (3.10):
crf = (1 + r)t · r
(1 + r)t −1
(3.10)
where r is the interest rate of the investment and t is the lifetime of the project. The
operating expenditures (OPEX) cost of the production is divided into two parts. OPEXvar
is the annual variable OPEX that considers the cost of the feedstocks used for the process
and the cost of electricity and is calculated with eq. (3.11).
OPEXvar = CF · 8760 · ( ˙mCO2 · pCO2 + ˙VH2O · pH2O + (| ˙WSOEC + ˙WBOP |) · pel) (3.11)
Here, CF is the capacity factor of the electricity production that indicates the part of the
year that electricity is produced and therefore how many hours the SNG production system
operates. ˙mCO2 is the hourly demand of CO2 in tons, pCO2 is the price of CO2 in €/tCO2,
˙VH2O is the hourly demand of water in m3, pH2O is the price of water in €/m3 and pel is the
price of electricity given in €/MWh. The number 8760 stands for the number of hours in a
year.
The other part of the OPEX is the fixed annual OPEX (OPEXfix) that covers the cost of
maintaining the components of the system and exchanging catalysts and SOEC stacks
when required. The calculation of the OPEXfix is given with eq. (3.12):
OPEXfix = fSOEC · CAPEXSOEC + fCM · CAPEXCM
(3.12)
Here f stands for the percentage of the CAPEX of the technologies that will cover the
needed operational cost to keep the system running.
ESNG stands for the annual production of SNG in MWh and is calculated with eq. (3.13)
ESNG = CF · 8760 · ˙mSNG · LHVSNG
(3.13)
3.4
Process design
This section will cover the description of the three designed systems and a short descrip­
tion of the software used for the simulations. Each system will have its own section where
the process is described and the process design parameters are defined. Process flow
diagrams of all three systems will be displayed in section 4.1.
3.4.1
Software
The systems are designed and modeled in the open­source software: ”Dynamic Network
Analysis” (DNA). DNA is a zero­dimensional component­based thermodynamic modeling
and simulation tool developed by DTU Mechanical Engineering. The software can be
used to simulate and analyze energy systems, and the mass and energy conservation
is automatically taken into account [32]. All the components used in the design of the
systems are already available in DNA.
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
17
==PAGE:31==
3.4.2
Process components and conditions of inlet and outlet streams
The main components of the PtG systems are the SOEC and the catalytic reactors. The
type of reactors used for the simulations is Gibbs reactors that calculate the equilibrium
composition of the outlet stream with the minimization of the Gibbs free energy. The rest
of the systems consist of auxiliary components that have the same process design param­
eters in all three systems. An overview of the process design parameters of the auxiliary
components is listed in table 3.1. Table 3.2 indicates the temperature and pressure of the
main streams that enter and exit the systems.

3.4.3
Atmospheric operation of SOEC with downstream methanation
This system is based on SOEC operation where hydrogen is formed from water electrol­
ysis at atmospheric pressure. The hydrogen is then mixed with CO2 to form syngas that
is pressurized and fed to a methanation process where the desired SNG is produced. A
more detailed description of the process is given here below.
The water that enters the system, at the given conditions in table 3.2, is evaporated and
heated to reach the set inlet temperature of the SOEC. In order to reach the 700 °C,
heat from other streams and components in the system is utilized. First, the water goes
through a gas cooler where it acts as a cooling media, and water is condensed out of a
methane­rich stream. The heat exchanged in the gas cooler is sufficient enough to bring
the water to boiling point. Then it goes to an evaporator that utilizes the heat generated in
18
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:32==
the first methanation reactor and heat from the hot oxygen that is formed on the oxygen
electrode on the SOEC.
After the evaporation, the stream is led through three heat exchangers. In the first heat
exchanger, the steam exchanges heat with the output gas of the first methanation reactor.
The second heat exchange is with the part of the hydrogen­rich stream that comes out of
the SOEC. The third heat exchanger draws the heat from the hot oxygen stream from the
SOEC. The steam is then mixed with a part of the output stream of the fuel electrode of
the SOEC in order to have 10% (molar­based) hydrogen in the inlet stream of the SOEC.
The hydrogen is needed to create a reducing environment at the fuel electrode to prohibit
oxidation of the nickel [9]. The available heat in the system is not sufficient enough to
get the steam to 700 °C so an eclectic heater is used to heat the steam to the desired
temperature.
The stream goes into the SOEC, which has an isothermal operation. The ASR of the
SOEC is set to be 0.4 Ωcm2 and the utilization factor is also set to 0.8. The output of
the SOEC is a hydrogen­rich stream that is split up. One part is mixed with the steam
before entering the SOEC, as mentioned earlier. The other part is cooled to saturation
temperature in two steps. The first part of the cooling is a part of the heating of the steam
and the rest of the cooling is provided with a heat sink. The stream is then further cooled
down to 25 °C and simultaneously the water in the stream condenses and is extracted
from the stream. The dehydrated stream is then mixed with the fixed amount of CO2
specified in section 3.1. The mixing results in a stream with the H2/CO2 ratio of 4. The
stream is then compressed to 10 bars and then led to the first methanation reactor that is
operated isothermally.
Water is formed in the first methanation reactor and to get better operation in the rest
of the system, the water must be condensed out of the stream. Therefore, the stream
is cooled down to saturation temperature. The cooling of the stream corresponds to the
heat exchange with the steam right after evaporation. The stream then goes to the gas
cooler, which is used to heat the water to boiling point, where the water in the stream is
condensed out and the outlet temperature of the gas is 30 °C. The gas is then heated to
210 °C before it goes to the second methanation reactor. That reactor is adiabatic so all
the heat generated is used to increase the temperature of the gas. The hot gas out of the
second methanation reactor is used as the heat source for the heating of the gas after the
gas cooler.
Now the majority, if not all, of the carbon supplied to the system is converted to methane,
and the rest of the stream is mainly water. The water is extracted from the stream in a
dehydration unit resulting in a pure SNG stream. That stream is then compressed and
cooled in order to get the temperature and pressure stated in table 3.2.
The oxygen side of the SOEC has not been described. During water electrolysis, it is not
required that a stream is supplied to the oxygen electrode. In this case, the only stream
that is related to the oxygen electrode is the oxygen that is formed. The stream of oxygen
exits the SOEC at the same temperature and pressure as the hydrogen­rich stream. Like
stated before this hot stream is utilized for heating and evaporation. The oxygen will not
be considered a product that can be sold and will just be released to the environment after
the needed heat has been extracted from it.
Overview of the process design parameters for the SOEC and the downstream methana­
tion are displayed in table 3.3 and table 3.4.
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis

3.4.4
Pressurized operation of SOEC with downstream methanation
The main difference between this system and the system described in section 3.4.3 is
that the SOEC water electrolysis is performed under elevated pressure. A more detailed
description is given here below.
The stream that enters the SOEC in this design is at 700 °C and 10 bar. The pressurization
of the water will happen before any heating of the water occurs. The water that enters
the system is directly led to a pump where the pressure is increased to the operating
pressure of the SOEC. Then the pressurized water is fed to a gas cooler where it is used
as a cooling media for the condensation of the water in the methane­rich stream from the
first methanation reactor. The heat exchanged in the gas cooler is not sufficient to heat
the pressurized water to boiling point. Therefore, the water first exchanges heat with the
output stream of the first methanation reactor to reach boiling point before it is led to an
evaporator. The evaporation utilizes the heat from the first methanation reactor and heat
from the hot oxygen stream that is formed in the SOEC.
Now the water is in the form of steam that needs further heating to reach the desired
temperature. The steam first exchanges heat with a part of the outlet stream from the fuel
side of the SOEC and then there is heat exchanged with the direct output from the oxygen
side of the SOEC. The mixing of steam with a part of the product stream of the SOEC
is also relevant here for the same reasons as in the system with the atmospheric SOEC
operation. At this point, the stream has not reached the desired temperature and all the
high­temperature heat available in the system has been utilized. Therefore an electric
heater is required to heat up the stream to the operating temperature of the SOEC. The
20
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:34==
process design parameters for the SOEC are almost identical to the one defined for the
atmospheric pressure operation but the increased pressure is the only difference.
The part of the hydrogen­rich steam exiting the SOEC that is not mixed with the steam
is cooled to 210 °C, but as stated before the heat extracted is used to heat up the steam
before the SOEC. Now the stream is ready to be mixed with the CO2 that is supplied to the
system and the H2/CO2 ratio is 4. Before the two streams are mixed the CO2 needs to
be compressed to the same pressure as the hydrogen­rich stream, as gaseous streams
cannot be mixed with different pressures. Note that the hydrogen stream is not cooled
down to extract the water in the stream. The reason is that if the stream is cooled too
far, some heating is required to heat the mixed stream with hydrogen and CO2 before it
enters the first methanation reactor.
Here the first methanation reactor is not operated isothermally and the output temperature
is set to 300°C. Some water is formed in the methanation reactor and it is desired to extract
it from the stream before further processing. Therefore, the stream is cooled down to
saturation temperature in two steps. First, the stream is cooled down in a heat exchanger
(heating the pressurized water to boiling point) and then the rest of the cooling is supplied
with a heat sink. Now that the stream is at saturation temperature the water is condensed
in the gas cooler, as it exchanges heat with the pressurized water discussed earlier. The
following steps of the process regarding the SNG production are identical to the system
with the atmospheric SOEC operation.
The oxygen produced at the SOEC is also used here as a heat source but here it is pres­
surized so before it is released to the environment it is led to a turbine to produce electricity
that can be utilized by the process. An overview of the process design parameters for the
pressurized SOEC and downstream methanation are displayed in table 3.5 and table 3.6.

3.4.5
Pressurized co­electrolysis of SOEC with downstream methanation
This design will be based on the pressurized co­electrolysis operation of the SOEC. With
the co­electrolysis, some additions are needed for the operation compared to the other
two systems, but that is mainly related to the oxygen side of the SOEC. To gain better
understanding of the design of the system, a detailed description is provided here below.
Since the SOEC is operated at pressurized conditions the streams fed to it need to be
pressurized. Therefore, the same method is used here as in the pressurized water elec­
trolysis system. The water is led to a pump that increases the pressure of the water to
the relevant pressure. The pressurized water then goes through a gas cooler where it is
the cooling media for the condensation of water in a methane­rich stream. The heat ex­
change in the gas cooler is not sufficient enough to bring the pressurized water to boiling
point so further heating is required before the evaporation. To reach the boiling point the
water exchanges heat with the output of the first methanation reactor. As the water is at
boiling point it can be evaporated by utilizing the heat from the first methanation reactor
and the output on the oxygen side of the SOEC.
Before the steam is mixed with the CO2, that is supplied to the system, it exchanges heat
with the part of the product stream of the SOEC that is used for the SNG production. The
CO2 is compressed before it is mixed with the steam due to mixing principles. Now the
mixed stream goes into a heat exchanger with the same stream that provides part of the
heat required for the evaporation. However, the heat exchange occurs before the heat
is extracted for the evaporation. The hot stream of steam and CO2 is then mixed with a
part of the product stream of the SOEC to create a reducing environment at the inlet of
the fuel electrode. Here external heating from an electric heater is not required to reach
the operation temperature of the SOEC.
The operation of the SOEC is quite different from the other systems. Here the SOEC is
not operated isothermally. The main reason is the exothermic characteristic of the internal
formation of methane in the SOEC. The SOEC is designed to have a 50° temperature
difference between the inlet and the outlet of the electrodes. The ASR is the same as in
the other systems. The H/C ratio needs to be considered to avoid the formation of solid
carbon at the fuel electrode. The H/C in the system is set to be 6.20. Based on fig. 2.7
that ratio is high enough to avoid carbon formation at both inlet and outlet temperature of
the SOEC.
The part of the product stream that is not recycled will be the feed for the machination
upgrading. The rest of the process concerning the SNG production is almost identical to
the system based on pressurized SOEC water electrolysis. The water is condensed out
of the methane­rich stream that then is led to the second methanation reactor to convert
22
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:36==
unconverted carbon oxides to methane. The water formed in the second methanation
reactor is extracted from the stream and the SNG is then prepared for injection to the
grid.
The part that is different here from the other systems is that the oxygen electrode has an in­
let and outlet stream. Oxygen­enriched air is supplied to the oxygen electrode and is used
as a sweep gas to increase the performance of the SOEC. The stream that is supplied
to the oxygen electrode is a mixture of compressed air and a part of the oxygen­enriched
air that exits the oxygen electrode. An ejector is used to combine the two streams. The
oxygen­enriched air that is not recycled is utilized as a heat source as mentioned before.
After the needed heat has been extracted from the stream it is led to a turbine where it is
expanded to atmospheric pressure and electricity is produced that is then utilized by the
system. An overview of the process design parameters for the SOEC and downstream
methanation is displayed in table 3.7 and table 3.8.
Table 3.7: Process design parameters for SOEC co­electrolysis at elevated pressure.

Results and discussion
4.1
Technical evaluation
This section covers the evaluation of the technical aspects of the systems. The results
from the simulation of each system based on the process design parameters defined in
section 3.4 are displayed, along with the efficiencies stated in section 3.2. Each system
will be analysed in its own section and at last, there will be a section that gathers the
results and compares the systems.
4.1.1
Atmospheric operation of SOEC with downstream methanation
A process flow diagram of the system is displayed in fig. 4.1 and table 4.5 contains the
information about mass­flows and thermodynamic properties of the streams in the system
that are generated by the model based on the set design parameters.
Information on the composition and LHV of the main streams in the system is found in
table 4.1. The electric power consumption of the components in the system is shown in
table 4.2. That table indicates that the SOEC is the main power consumer in the sys­
tem with almost 94% of all the consumption. The overall electricity to SNG efficiency is
calculated to be 73.21 % based on the LHV of the SNG produced.

The software also calculates the operating parameters and the active cell area of the
SOEC and the results are reported in table 4.4. The SOEC is operated isothermally and in
order to keep the isothermal conditions heat is generated by the internal resistance. This
results in a higher current density and therefore higher operating voltage of the system.
However, the current density and the operating voltage can be reduced by operating the
system with the AC:DC operation described in section 2.1.3. The required heat for the
isothermal operation is then obtained by the short­term fuel cell operation of the SOEC
that occurs at fixed time intervals. Additionally the AC:DC operation reduces degradation
of the cells resulting in longer lifetime and less frequent stack changes. The active cell
area of the system will be used in the economic evaluation.

4.1.3
Pressurized co­electrolysis of SOEC with downstream methanation
This system is more complex than those based on water electrolysis. The main reason is
the air sweep on the oxygen side of the SOEC that adds a compressor and an ejector to
the system. Figure 4.3 is a process flow diagram of the system and table 4.15 provides
information on the mass­flows and thermodynamic properties of the stream in the system.
Table 4.11 shows the molar composition and the LHV of the streams in the system. The
methane molar fraction in the output of the fuel side of the SOEC is 26.98%. This indicates
that after the co­electrolysis a large part of the methane in the end product is already
formed during the electrolysis. At the outlet of the SOEC around 72% of the carbon in the
stream is already in the form of methane.
The electric power consumption and generation are shown in table 4.12. The electric
power consumption of the BOP mainly corresponds to the air compressor in relation to
the air sweep on the oxygen side of the SOEC. However, a part of the oxygen­rich stream
that exits the SOEC is led to a turbine, after the needed heat has been extracted from it, to
generate electricity. The electricity produced by the turbine is sufficient enough to power
the air compressor and a small part of the BOP. The SOEC is responsible for 98.6% of
the external electricity consumed. The calculated electricity to SNG efficiency is 82.0%
based on the LHV of the SNG.

fraction out indicates the total share of H2O and CO2 in the outlet stream and the O2
fraction out indicates the share of oxygen in the output on the oxygen side.

4.1.4
Technical comparison of the systems
This section will cover the technical comparison of the three proposed systems on a tech­
nical level. The main thing to look at is the electricity to SNG efficiency but table 4.16
shows the efficiencies for all three systems. The best efficiency is obtained in the co­
electrolysis system and the PtG efficiency is 82.0%. The system that is based on the
pressurized water electrolysis shows better efficiency than the system with water electrol­
ysis at atmospheric pressure.
Table 4.16: Efficiency comparison.
ATM
Pressurized
Co­electrolysis
ηPtG
0.7321
0.7630
0.8200
ηSOEC
0.9371
0.9369
0.8848
Table 4.17 displays the molar composition and the LHV of the SNG streams that are
formed in the three systems. The streams mainly consist of methane and the LHV is quite
close in all cases. However, it can be seen that the LHV of the SNG that is produced in
the system based on the atmospheric operation of the SOEC, is a bit higher than in the
other systems. The reason for the higher value is that the H2/CO2 ratio that goes into the
methanation process is a bit higher than the optimal value of 4. Which results in excess
hydrogen that can not be converted to methane, since all the carbon is used up. The
hydrogen content in the SNG stream is just 3% of the molar composition but that is within
the limit that is 5% [33]. The increased hydrogen also leads to higher steam intake to the
SOEC so it is also likely that the power demand by the SOEC is slightly increased.
Table 4.17: Comparison of end product streams of the systems.
Molar composition

The electric power consumption and generation of each case are reported in table 4.18.
With increased pressure in the operation of the SOEC, the total electric power consump­
tion of BOP decreases. That is mainly due to the turbine that expands the hot oxygen
stream that comes from the oxygen side of the SOEC to generate electricity. Another
observation in regard to the BOP is that pressurizing the water as a liquid requires far
less power than compressing it as a gas. This can be seen by looking at the sum of the
power requirement of the water pump, the syngas compressor, and the CO2 compres­
sor. Those components are responsible for getting the streams to the desired pressure.
Pumping liquid is way more efficient than compressing gas so there is clearly a benefit
here to have the water pressurized in the liquid form before it is evaporated. However,
pressurizing the stream leads to increased heat requirement to heat up the stream to high
temperatures but that is not an issue for the systems due to the exothermic process of
methane formation. The syngas compressor in the atmospheric case also consumes way
more power for compression. The reason is that it takes care of the compression of the
CO2 and the hydrogen­rich stream from the SOEC. The other two cases only compress
the CO2 but the water was pressurized before it was evaporated.
36
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:50==
The power requirement for the SOEC does not differ much between the systems with the
water electrolysis and if the H2/CO2 ratio was ideal for the atmospheric case then it is likely
that the requirement would be closer. For the co­electrolysis, the power requirement is
around 7% less than in the systems based on the pressurized water electrolysis. Overall
the co­electrolysis case requires the least power, in both BOP and SOEC, to produce the
desired SNG.

Information on the operating conditions of all three systems is given in table 4.19. The
operating voltage of the systems is almost the same, if not the same. The reversible
voltage is a bit higher for the pressurized water electrolysis than in the atmospheric case.
That is in line with what is stated in section 2.1.3 where the reversible voltage slightly
increases with increased pressure. The reversible voltage for the co­electrolysis is also
not far from the pressurized system. The difference between the operating voltage and the
reversible voltage indicates the losses, or the overpotential, of the systems. The reversible
voltage increases with increased pressure but the operating voltage is barely affected by
the pressure change. That indicates that operating at elevated pressures decreases the
losses of the SOEC. One of the main reasons why the losses are decreased is that the
internal resistance decreases with increased pressure. However, the ASR is fixed to 0.4
Ωcm2 in all three systems. As the ASR is fixed the current density in the system based
on the pressurized SOEC is reduced, compared to the one in the atmospheric system,
in order to reach the operating voltage calculated by the simulation. A more realistic
approach would be to have a lower ASR for the pressurized system. Then the current
density would not differ as much based on operating pressure. The current density in the
co­electrolysis system does not need to be as high as in the other systems since the heat
in the system is mainly obtained from the exothermic internal formation of methane in the
cell.

4.1.5
Technical comparison with other PtG studies
Table 4.20 gives an overview of PtG systems, based on SOEC, that have been analyzed.
It displays the efficiencies calculated by this study as well as some results from other
studies. The efficiency calculated for the system based on the atmospheric operation of
SOEC shows slightly lower efficiency than the system analyzed by Qi et al. [34]. The
efficiency found for the system based on the pressurized operation of the SOEC find the
efficiency of the process to be quite close to the results obtained by Giglio et al. [10] and
Salomone et al. [14]. The efficiency of the co­electrolysis system in this study shows a
similar result to the one Giglio et al. [10] found for a PtG system based on co­electrolysis
of SOEC. However, there is a large gap between the efficiency found in this study and the
results obtained by Ebbehøj [13]. The reason for the low efficiency calculated by Ebbehøj
is that the electric consumption of the direct air capture unit is included in the calculation.

4.2
Economic evaluation
This section covers the economic evaluation of the systems. Each system will be analyzed
in its own section and a special section will cover the comparison of the results of systems.
The economic calculations utilize results from the simulations of the models to determine
the demand for feedstock and electricity, as well as the energy output of the system. The
simulations also generate information about the size of the SOEC and the downstream
catalytic methanation process. The cost of the feedstocks used for the process is needed
for the analysis. The cost of water is set to be 2.12 €/m3 [35]. The CO2 utilized by the
system can be sourced from different technologies and the three sources considered in
38
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:52==
the analysis are bioenergy with carbon capture and storage (BECCS), carbon capture
from industrial processes (flue gas), and direct air capture (DAC) of CO2. Table 4.21
shows the different prices of CO2 based on the carbon capture technology.
Table 4.21: Cost of CO2 based on the carbon capture technology.
BECCS [36]
Flue gas [37]
DAC [36]

33.75

The source of electricity for the systems can also originate from different sources. The
sources of electricity considered in the analysis are offshore wind, onshore wind, and pho­
tovoltaic (PV). The levelized cost of electricity (LCOE) and the capacity factor (CF) differ
between the electricity generation technologies. The LCOE and CF for the technologies
are displayed in table 4.22 and the numbers are based on a weather profile with high wind
capacity but low solar capacity [38].
Table 4.22: LCOE and CF for the different electricity generation technologies, based on

As stated in section 3.3 the OPEX of the systems is divided into fixed and variable OPEX.
The annual fixed OPEX is a percentage of the total CAPEX of each technology. The
percentage used for the fixed annual OPEX of the SOEC is 5% [39] and the percentage
of the catalytic methanation is 10% [40]. The discount factor, assumed here to be the
weighted average cost of capital (WACC), is set to be 10% but that is in line with the
study made by Gerloff in 2023 [35] on the levelized cost of PtG generation in Germany.
The lifetime of the system is assumed to be 25 years and the start of operation is in the
year 2030. The specific investment cost of the SOEC is 1.061 €/kW but that specific
number is valid for a SOEC with 5 MW capacity. In order to get the valid CAPEX for
the size of the SOEC in the systems the economy of scale needs to be considered by
applying eq. (3.5). This is also valid for the calculation of the CAPEX for the catalytic
methanation where the specific investment cost is 440 €/kW for a system with 5 MWSNG
output. The scaling factor for the SOEC is 0.79 and 0.76 for the catalytic methanation
[39]. The results displayed in the sections below will be based on CO2 sourced from
BECCS and the electricity generated will come from offshore wind. The results from other
combinations of CO2 source and electricity generation are displayed in the appendix.
Table 4.23 provides an overview of the values used for the economic evaluation of the
systems presented here below.

Atmospheric operation of SOEC with downstream methanation

It was stated in section 3.3 that it is more relevant to look at the specific investment cost of
the SOEC as a function of cell area than as a function of capacity. The specific investment
cost per square meter is calculated by eq. (3.6) to be 5409.28 €/m2. The LCOP of the
system is calculated to be 145.25 €/MWhSNG.

A sensitivity analysis was performed for the LCOP where each variable was varied one
at a time while the other variables were kept constant. The sensitivity analysis results are
presented in fig. 4.4 as a tornado diagram. The variable that affects the LCOP the most is
placed at the top of the diagram and the variable that has the least effect is placed at the
bottom. The analysis indicates that the LCOP is most sensitive to the price of electricity
but a 10% increase or decrease in the electricity price can increase or reduce the LCOP
by almost 7%. The variable that comes after the electricity price is the capacity factor.
Increased capacity factor results in a lower LCOP which is logical as it enables more
production of SNG. The capacity factor is obtained from the electricity production and as
it increases more electricity can be produced. Therefore, a higher capacity factor might
also result in decreased LCOE of the electricity generation. It is noted that the variation
of the capacity factor in the sensitivity analysis does not affect the electricity price. The
change in the CAPEX of the SOEC and the WACC can also slightly affect the LCOP but
the rest of the variables have minimal effect on the LCOP.

spheric SOEC operation.

more sensitive to the decrease in the capacity factor than the increase. Other variables
that have a significant effect on the LCOP are the CAPEX of the SOEC and the WACC.

4.2.3
Pressurized co­electrolysis of SOEC with downstream methanation
The calculation for the co­electrolysis process differs slightly from the two other cases.
Since a large share of the methane is formed in the SOEC, a smaller downstream cat­
alytic process is required. The size is determined in a simplified way and will be based
on the formation of methane in the downstream process. Around 30% of the methane is
formed in the downstream process and that corresponds to around 30 MWSNG. There­
fore, the capacity of the downstream catalytic methanation is assumed to be 30 MWSNG.
This approach is not completely accurate as the 30 MWSNG sizing does not consider
the volume of the gas going through the process. A more realistic cost would be slightly
higher than the cost used in the calculations but since the source of the CAPEX is on
plant size level the approximation displayed here above is used. The values needed for
the calculation of the LCOP for the co­electrolysis case are given in table 4.26 leading to
a LCOP of 143.02 €/MWhSNG.
Table 4.26: Values for the economic evaluation of the system based on co­electrolysis.

Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis

Figure 4.6 displays the tornado diagram of a sensitivity analysis that was performed on
the LCOP of the system. The results show that LCOP is most sensitive to the electricity
price but a 10% change in the price of electricity leads to a 6% change in the LCOP. The
variables that follow the electricity price are the capacity factor and the CAPEX of the
SOEC, where a higher capacity factor and lower CAPEX of the SOEC are beneficial as
the LCOP decreases. The WACC and the fixed OPEX concerning the SOEC can slightly
affect the LCOP. Changes in cost related to the downstream catalytic methanation and
the feedstock cost have close to no effect on the LCOP.

and compare the results. As the main focus of the economic evaluation is the LCOP of
the systems the results for the LCOP of the systems are displayed in table 4.27. The
difference between the lowest and the highest LCOP of the systems is not much as it
is only 4.83 €/MWhSNG. The co­electrolysis system offers the lowest LCOP of 143.02
€/MWhSNG and the pressurized water electrolysis system gives the highest LCOP of
147.86 €/MWhSNG.

147.85
143.02
Figure 4.7 also indicates the LCOP of the systems and also provides the breakdown of
the production cost in percentages. There it can be seen that the electricity cost is the
main contributor to the cost of production for all systems. The cost of feedstock is only
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
43
==PAGE:57==
a small share of the production cost and the share of OPEX in the systems is similar in
all systems. The CAPEX is the highest for the co­electrolysis system and it is lowest for
the atmospheric SOEC operation. On the other hand, the cost of electricity is the highest
for the atmospheric SOEC operation and lowest for the co­electrolysis. This is in line with
the results from the technical evaluation where the co­electrolysis system has the largest
cell area of SOEC and the lowest electric power demand and the other way around for
the atmospheric SOEC operation.
Figure 4.7: LCOP breakdown.
As the co­electrolysis system has the lowest LCOP it is relevant to see how it compares to
the assumed prices of natural gas in 2030. The Danish Energy Agency predicts the price
of natural gas in 2030 to be around 30 €/MWh [41]. That price is far from the calculated
price for the SNG produced and even if the electricity price is set to a value close to zero
it is almost double the estimated price of NG in 2030. Figure 4.8 indicates the LCOP for
the co­electrolysis system based on different electricity prices.
Figure 4.8: LCOP for the co­electrolysis based on the LCOE.
44
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
==PAGE:58==
To compare the sensitivity of the three systems the impact of changing the electricity price
and the SOEC CAPEX have been gathered in table 4.28. The SOEC CAPEX has been
chosen instead of the capacity factor due to the fact that it is not dependent on the geo­
graphical location of the power production. All systems are most sensitive to the change
in the price of electricity which is logical since it is the main cost factor in the production
cost. However, as the electric power demand decreases the less sensitive the system is
to changes in electricity prices. The same can be said for the SOEC CAPEX where the
systems that have lower CAPEX due to less cell area needed are less sensitive to the
changes in SOEC CAPEX.

As the LCOP is dependent on several factors fig. 4.9 indicates a range of LCOP for each
system instead of a specific value. The LCOP range is generated by calculating the LCOP
for each system in two different scenarios. The upper limit of the range is obtained by
increasing both the electricity price and the SOEC CAPEX by 10%. The lower limit is
then calculated by decreasing both the electricity price and the SOEC CAPEX by 10%.
Interestingly, the difference in the higher and the lower values in all cases is almost the
same.
Figure 4.9: Range of production cost based on increased or decreased LCOE and SOEC
CAPEX.
4.2.5
Economical comparison with other PtG studies
The LCOP of the systems analyzed in this system is displayed in table 4.29 along with
the production cost of other PtG studies that are based on SOEC. The comparison of
production costs of different PtG plants can be difficult as the systems can have different
process designs. The source of electricity is also an important factor when determining
the LCOP.
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
45
==PAGE:59==
The LCOP calculated in this study for the system based on the atmospheric SOEC op­
eration is around 10% lower than the results obtained by Qi et al. [34]. However, that
system is a PtG process that is integrated with an electricity storage unit that utilizes the
stored energy and sells electricity to the grid at peak hours. This configuration of a PtG
plant generates lower LCOP at higher electricity prices due to the revenues from selling
electricity back to the grid at peak hours.
The LCOP for the PtG plant based on pressurized water electrolysis by Salomone et
al. [14] is given in the range of 60.2­224.9 €/MWhSNG. The lower limit of the range
corresponds to low electricity prices based on surplus electric energy and the upper limit
takes the price for deficit electricity which is set to be 50.9 €/MWh. The upper limit is way
higher than the LCOP calculated for the system based on pressurized water electrolysis
in this study even though the electricity price is considerably lower.
Ebbehøj [13] calculated the LCOP for a PtG plant, based on co­electrolysis and DAC unit,
to be 190.0 €/MWhSNG. The electricity price used for the calculation is similar to the one
used in this study. A probable reason for the higher price obtained by Ebbehøj is the
cost of the DAC unit that is included in the calculations. The LCOP calculations made by
Giglio et al. [42] find the production cost of the two systems to be similar. The calculated
LCOP for the systems is around 84.3 €/MWhSNG based on an electricity price of around
60 €/MWh. This LCOP is way lower than the ones calculated by this study. The reason
for the lower cost is that the CAPEX and OPEX are significantly lower.

==PAGE:60==
5
Conclusion
A techno­economic analysis of three different configurations of a PtG system based on
SOEC electrolysis, with downstream methanation, has been carried out in this study. The
focus of the analysis is to compare the performance of the systems on technical and eco­
nomic levels. The systems are modeled in a component­based, zero­dimensional tool
called DNA to generate the values needed for the analysis. The technical performance
of the systems is measured in the electricity to SNG efficiency, ηPtG. The economic com­
parison is based on the levelized cost of production of SNG.
Two of the PtG systems produce hydrogen, with steam electrolysis, that is then mixed
with CO2 before the methanation process. The operating pressure of the SOEC is the
part that sets the systems apart as one is operated at atmospheric pressure and the other
at 10 bar. The third system operates the SOEC in co­electrolysis mode as it is fed with
CO2 and steam. The co­electrolysis is operated at 18 bar resulting in internal methane
formation in the SOEC where around 70% of the carbon supplied to the system is already
converted to methane.
The system based on the co­electrolysis operation achieved the highest electricity to SNG
efficiency of 82.0%. Higher efficiency is obtained by the system based on the pressurized
steam electrolysis as it decreases the electric power consumption of the balance­of­plant
components. The reported efficiency for the system based on pressurized steam electrol­
ysis is 76.3% while the efficiency for the system based on atmospheric steam electrolysis
is 73.2%.
The required active cell area of the SOEC increases with increased pressure resulting in
higher CAPEX of the system. However, the improved efficiency obtained by increased
pressure can offset the effect of higher CAPEX for the levelized cost of production. The
production cost does not differ much between all three systems. The system based on
the co­electrolysis has the lowest production cost of 143.02 €/MWhSNG even though the
system has the highest CAPEX. The system based on the pressurized operation of SOEC
has the highest production cost of 147.9 €/MWhSNG. The improved efficiency obtained by
the increased operating pressure does not manage to outweigh the increased CAPEX as
the production cost is higher than the system based on atmospheric steam electrolysis.
Sensitivity analysis evaluates how much the production cost is affected by changes in
the cost parameters. The analysis indicates the production cost is most vulnerable to
changes in the price of electricity. The electricity is also the main contributor to the cost
as it is responsible for more than 60% of the total production cost in all three systems.
The technical and economic evaluation of the three systems shows different efficiencies
and production costs for the three systems. Taking the results from both evaluations in­
dicates that the system based on the co­electrolysis shows the most potential as it has
the highest electricity to SNG efficiency and the lowest production cost. However, the
production cost is way higher than the assumed price of natural gas, 30 €/MWh in 2030.
Therefore, the produced SNG is not competitive with natural gas based on the cost pa­
rameters used for the production cost calculations.
For further work, a more detailed and improved model of the PtG process can be devel­
oped. The use of humidifiers in the system based on the co­electrolysis could reduce the
heat required for the evaporation of water resulting in better efficiency. Another thought
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
47
==PAGE:61==
for further work is to have the PtG system connected to the electric grid and see how the
operation copes with the fluctuating prices and see if it is relevant to coordinate an EES
system with that operation.
48
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis

B.3
Codes for the system based on pressurized operation on
SOEC
title Pressurized_system
C ----- Defina a stream for the CO2 -------
fluid CO2-stored H2 0.0 CO2 1.0 CO 0.0 CH4 0.0
C
------ Additional mixer to have some CO_2 in stream to make SOEC work --
C
------ CO2 fraction set to 0.0000001
struc mix_1 mixer_02 66 1 2
media 2 H2O-for-SOEC
media 66 CO2-stored
struc set_temp1 SET_TEMP 2 3 920
struc set-CO2 SET_X 3 4 6
addco za set-CO2 1 0.0000001
C ------ Split of output from the fuel side ----
struc fuel-split splitter 8 70 9
struc valve8 PRES_SEP 9 10
C ------ Mixing of steam and part of the output from fuel side---
C ------ 10% H2 desired in the stream
struc H2-mix mixer_01 4 10 5
media 5 H2O-and-H2-to-SOEC
STRUC set_H2 SET_X 5 6 1
addco za set_H2 1 0.1
C ------ Electric heater to get stream to 700°C ---
struc e-heater heatsrc0 6 7 304 0
addco t e-heater 7 700
C
******** SOEC ********
media 8 USEDFUEL 31 USEDAIR
fluid luft O2 1
media 30 luft
struc soec sofceq0d_CBM_LRC2 /
{fuel and air inlets} 7 30 /
{fuel and air outlets} 8 31 /
{nodes for power and heat loss} 201 301 302 901 902 /
{pressure loss ratios} 0.1 0.0
addco m soec 30 0
addco q soec 301 0
addco t soec 30 700
addco p 31 10
addco p 7 10.1
68
Techno­economic comparison of Power­To­Gas systems based on SOEC electrolysis
